                    "Text": "=== Operation 1 Result (Text) ===\n# Claude Cost Management\n\nReference for controlling token spend when using Claude in autonomous loops.\n\n## Model Pricing (as of 2026)\n\n| Model | Input $/1M | Output $/1M | Best for |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| Haiku 4.5 | $1.00 | $5.00 | Reading, searching, classification |\n| Sonnet 4.6 | $3.00 | $15.00 | Code generation, editing, tests |\n| Opus 4.6 | $5.00 | $25.00 | Debugging, architecture, reasoning |\n\n**Rule of thumb:** Use the cheapest model that can complete the task without stalling. Opus costs 5× Haiku on input and 5× on output — reserve it for tasks where Sonnet has already failed.\n\n## Prompt Caching\n\nRepeated context (system prompts, practices files, specs) is the largest cost driver in loops.\n\n**How it works:**\n- Add `cache_control: {type: \"ephemeral\", ttl: \"1h\"}` to large content blocks\n- Cached input tokens cost ~10% of normal input rate\n- Cache TTL defaults to 5 minutes — too short for loop iterations that run every 10–30 minutes\n- Always set `ttl: \"1h\"` for context shared across loop iterations\n\n**What to cache:**\n- `specs/prd.md` (rarely changes, loaded every iteration)\n- `practices/*.md` files (stable reference material)\n- Large tool definitions or schema blocks repeated across turns\n\n**What not to cache:**\n- The current task description (changes each iteration)\n- `specs/plan.md` (updated after each task — stale cache would confuse the agent)\n- Short content blocks under ~500 tokens (caching overhead isn't worth it)\n\n## Subagent Economics\n\nEach subagent is a separate API call with its own context window. Costs multiply.\n\n**Caps that keep costs predictable:**\n- Reading/searching: ≤3 Haiku subagents, ≤5 turns each\n- Code generation: 1 Sonnet subagent, ≤20 turns\n- Build/test: 1 Sonnet subagent (prevents parallelising around failures)\n- Reasoning: 1 Opus subagent (only when cheaper models stall)\n\n**Kill signals:** If a subagent exceeds 10 turns on a simple read task, it is stuck. Terminate it, note the failure in activity.md, and use a simpler approach next iteration.\n\n## Batches API\n\nFor non-interactive work (bulk classification, report generation, batch analysis), the Batches API charges 50% of standard rates.\n\n- Up to 100,000 requests or 256 MB per batch\n- Results available within 24 hours (most complete in 1 hour)\n- Use for: generating fixtures, bulk document processing, offline analysis tasks\n- Not suitable for: the real-time build loop itself (interactive tasks need synchronous responses)\n\n## Token Counting\n\nCount tokens before expensive calls to catch surprises early:\n\n```python\ncount = client.messages.count_tokens(model=\"claude-opus-4-6\", messages=messages)\n# Abort or truncate if count.input_tokens > your budget threshold\n```\n\nUse token counting in CI gates to alert when context grows unexpectedly large.\n\n## Effort Parameter\n\nControl reasoning depth and cost via `output_config: {effort: \"low\"|\"medium\"|\"high\"|\"max\"}`:\n\n| Level | Use case |\n|---|---|\n| `low` | Simple reads, classification, boilerplate generation |\n| `medium` | Standard code generation |\n| `high` | Default — good for most tasks |\n| `max` | Opus 4.6 only — hardest debugging and architectural decisions |\n\nCombine with adaptive thinking (`thinking: {type: \"adaptive\"}`) for automatic depth scaling.\n\n## Compaction\n\nFor very long agent sessions that approach the 200K context window:\n\n- Enable with beta header `compact-2026-01-12` and `context_management: {edits: [{type: \"compact_20260112\"}]}`\n- The API summarises earlier context automatically when approaching the trigger threshold\n- **Critical:** append `response.content` (not just text) back to messages — compaction blocks must be preserved for the next request to work\n\nCompaction is most valuable for debugging sessions that accumulate many tool call results. For the build loop, keeping `specs/activity.md` trimmed (last 10 entries only) is a cheaper substitute.\n\n## Activity Log Hygiene\n\n`specs/activity.md` grows unboundedly and is loaded every iteration. Keep it short:\n\n- After each iteration, trim to the last 10 entries\n- Add a single summary line before the trimmed entries: `[Prior entries summarised: N iterations, key outcomes: ...]`\n- This keeps the file under ~2000 tokens regardless of project length\n\n=== Operation 2 Result (Text) ===\n# Prompt Language Patterns\n\nReference for anyone editing PROMPT_plan.md or PROMPT_build.md. These word choices are\nload-bearing — changing them degrades agent behavior in specific, documented ways.\n\n## Use These Words\n\n| Word / phrase | Why it matters |\n|---|---|\n| `study` | Implies deeper analysis than \"read\" or \"look at\" — the agent treats it as a directive to reason, not just retrieve |\n| `don't assume not implemented` | The most common loop failure mode is the agent inventing missing functionality rather than searching. This phrase directly counters it. |\n| `Ultrathink` | Triggers extended internal reasoning before acting. Use before high-stakes decisions: gap analysis, architectural choices, debugging. |\n| `capture the why` | Shapes commit messages and documentation toward intent rather than mechanics |\n| `up to N subagents` | Sets a ceiling without mandating a floor — the agent scales to the task |\n| `only 1 subagent for build/tests` | Enforces backpressure by preventing the agent from parallelising around a failing test |\n| `keep it up to date` | Without this, IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md drifts stale within a few iterations |\n| `resolve them or document them` | Prevents the agent from silently ignoring unrelated failures |\n\n## Avoid These Patterns\n\n- `read` or `look at` in place of `study` — lower signal, shallower analysis\n- Vague success criteria — if the agent can't tell when it's done, it won't stop\n- Long bullet lists of rules at the end of prompts — the agent front-loads attention; important rules belong near the top\n- Contradictory instructions — the agent will pick one and ignore the other unpredictably\n\n## Prompt Tuning\n\nTreat prompts like a feedback loop, not a one-time config:\n\n- When the agent fails in a specific, repeatable way — add one targeted line to the relevant prompt\n- When the agent adds unnecessary complexity — add a constraint, don't rewrite the whole prompt\n- When the plan goes stale or the agent seems confused about completion status — regenerate planning with `./loop.sh plan 1` rather than trying to patch the prompt\n\n## Cost Discipline\n\nToken spend compounds across iterations. These rules keep each loop iteration under $1.\n\n**Model selection (by task):**\n\n| Task | Model | Why |\n|---|---|---|\n| Reading files, searching, summarising | Haiku | Cheapest capable reader |\n| Code generation, editing, tests | Sonnet | Best cost/quality for generation |\n| Debugging, architecture, root-cause analysis | Opus | Justified only when cheaper models stall |\n\n**Subagent limits:**\n- Reading/searching: `up to 3 Haiku subagents`, each ≤5 turns\n- Build/test: `only 1 Sonnet subagent`\n- Kill any subagent that exceeds 10 turns — it is stuck, not thinking\n\n**Context loading rules:**\n- Load `specs/prd.md` and `specs/plan.md` directly via Read — no subagent needed\n- Only load framework/lang practices files relevant to the current task\n- Don't reload files already in context from the same iteration\n\n**Prompt caching:**\n- Large repeated context (prd.md, practices files) benefits from `cache_control: {type: \"ephemeral\", ttl: \"1h\"}`\n- The default 5-minute TTL expires between loop iterations; always specify `ttl: \"1h\"` for shared context blocks\n- Cached tokens cost ~10% of uncached input tokens — worth it for any block > 1000 tokens reused across turns"

Built-in git diff and blame — your GitLens replacement**\nZed has:\n- Gutter indicators (colored bars) showing changed/added/deleted lines inline\n- `cmd+shift+g` opens the git panel — staged/unstaged changes, commit message, commit button\n- Click any gutter indicator to see the inline diff hunk right there\n- `git blame` per line via hovering (enable with `\"git\": {\"inline_blame\": {\"enabled\": true}}` in settings)\n\nThis means after each loop iteration you can review exactly what changed without leaving Zed or opening GitHub. This is the integration point between Zed and the loop you're missing.\n\n**7. Workspace with multiple projects**\n`File → Add Folder to Project` adds a second root to your current workspace.                 "file_text": "# Zed Editor Practices\n\nPrinciples for using Zed effectively in the unpossible workflow. The goal is to keep\nthe loop running in the editor, review its output without leaving Zed, and write better\nspecs before the loop ever starts.\n\n## Core Setup\n\nAdd to your Zed `settings.json` (cmd+shift+p → \"zed: open settings\"):\n\n```json\n\"git\": {\n  \"inline_blame\": {\n    \"enabled\": true\n  }\n},\n\"terminal\": {\n  \"shell\": \"system\"\n}\n```\n\nInline blame shows who (or which loop iteration) last touched each line without opening\nthe git panel. Essential for reviewing loop output in spec files.\n\n## Running the Loop from Zed\n\nUse tasks instead of typing commands manually. Open with `cmd+shift+p` → \"task: spawn\".\n\n| Task | When to use |\n|---|---|\n| `loop: build (single, streaming)` | Reviewing one iteration live — output stays visible |\n| `loop: plan (single, streaming)` | Gap analysis before a build run — watch Claude's reasoning |\n| `loop: build (background)` | Letting the loop run unattended — terminal opens but doesn't steal focus |\n| `loop: plan (background)` | Same, for plan mode |\n| `sandbox: shell` | Dropping into the Docker sandbox for manual inspection |\n\nTasks are defined in `.zed/tasks.json` at the project root. Mirror any new Makefile\ntargets there — don't add tasks for things not already in the Makefile.\n\n## Terminal Layout\n\nZed's terminal panel (`cmd+j` to toggle) is sufficient for most loop work. For a\nnear-fullscreen terminal without leaving Zed:\n\n- Drag the terminal divider up until only the tab bar is visible above it\n- Or open a second Zed window (`cmd+shift+n`) dedicated to the running loop — one window\n  for specs/code, one for terminal output\n\nAvoid switching to iTerm mid-session. Context switching between apps breaks the review\nflow that Zed's split panes enable.\n\n## Reviewing Loop Output (Git)\n\nAfter each loop commit, review in Zed before touching anything else:\n\n1. `cmd+shift+g` opens the git panel — see all changed files at a glance\n2. Click any file in the panel to open its diff inline\n3. Gutter indicators (colored bars on the left edge) show changed lines in every open file\n4. To revert a single hunk: click the gutter indicator → \"Revert Hunk\"\n5. To revert the entire last commit: open the terminal and run `git reset --hard HEAD~1`\n\nDo not open the GitHub web UI to review loop commits. Everything you need is in the\ngit panel. The web UI is for sharing and PRs, not for iteration review.\n\n## Spec Writing Workflow\n\nThe most common cause of unintended loop behavior is specs that contradict each other\nor leave gaps the loop fills with assumptions. Zed's project search is the fix.\n\n**Before running the loop:**\n\n1. Open all active spec files in a split pane: `specs/prd.md` left, the relevant\n   activity spec right\n2. Use `cmd+shift+f` (project search) to search for any feature name or term you're\n   about to implement — verify it appears consistently across all spec files\n3. Look for the same term meaning different things in different files — that's the gap\n   the loop will exploit\n\n**Split pane layout for spec work:**\n- Left pane: `specs/prd.md` (constraints — language, framework, port)\n- Right pane: the activity spec you're currently writing\n- Terminal panel (collapsed): available via `cmd+j` when you need to run `plan1` to\n  check Claude's interpretation\n\n## Working Across Template and Project Simultaneously\n\nWhen porting improvements from unpossible (the template) to a project built from it:\n\n1. `File → Add Folder to Project` — add both roots to one Zed workspace\n2. Use `cmd+shift+f` to search across both — find where a practice or prompt pattern\n   needs updating in the project\n3. Use split panes to edit the template file and the project file side by side\n\nThis replaces the window-switching workflow and makes diffs between template and project\nvisible without a separate tool.\n\n## What Not to Do in Zed\n\n- Don't use the Zed agent panel for loop work — it's a separate context from the loop\n  and will confuse your mental model of what the agent knows\n- Don't run `loop.sh` directly in the terminal when a task exists for it — tasks keep\n  the command consistent and documented\n- Don't review loop output in the GitHub web UI during active development — it breaks\n  the edit→run→review cycle that Zed's git panel supports natively\n",

Essential for reviewing loop output in spec files.\n\n## Running the Loop from Zed\n\nUse tasks instead of typing commands manually. Open with `cmd+shift+p` → \"task: spawn\".\n\n| Task | When to use |\n|---|---|\n| `loop: build (single, streaming)` | Reviewing one iteration live — output stays visible |\n| `loop: plan (single, streaming)` | Gap analysis before a build run — watch Claude's reasoning |\n| `loop: build (background)` | Letting the loop run unattended — terminal opens but doesn't steal focus |\n| `loop: plan (background)` | Same, for plan mode |\n| `sandbox: shell` | Dropping into the Docker sandbox for manual inspection |\n\nTasks are defined in `.zed/tasks.json` at the project root. Context switching between apps breaks the review\nflow that Zed's split panes enable.\n\n## Reviewing Loop Output (Git)\n\nAfter each loop commit, review in Zed before touching anything else:\n\n1. `cmd+shift+g` opens the git panel — see all changed files at a glance\n2. Go sidecar binary**\nA small Go CLI that takes a file or directory and emits lint errors in a format Zed understands (LSP diagnostics or just stdout). You own the rules entirely. Ships as a single binary alongside unpossible 2.\n\n- Pro: no runtime dependency for users, fast, can do cross-file analysis (catch contradictions between `prd.md` and activity specs), fits the \"ships with the template\" model cleanly\n- Con: you have to build and maintain it, more upfront work\n- Best for: the cross-file contradiction detection that markdownlint can't do\n\n**3. Let me reflect back what I'm hearing to make sure we design the right thing:\n\n**What you're describing is essentially a doc compiler:**\n- Reads a defined structure spec (what sections must exist, what references must resolve)\n- Validates all markdown files against it\n- Auto-fixes what it can (formatting, broken cross-references, missing sections with stubs)\n- Flags what it can't fix (semantic gaps, contradictions) for human or LLM review\n- Runs in the Makefile as a pre-loop gate — loop doesn't start if lint fails\n\nThe \"save the LLM read steps\" insight is the key design principle. The sidecar's job isn't to replace LLM judgment — it's to eliminate the mechanical work so the LLM only touches things that genuinely require reasoning.\n\n---\n\nBefore I spec this out, a few sharp questions:\n\n**Scope:**\n\n1. When you say \"keep READMEs in sync with a code-driven process\" — do you mean things like: a README that lists available Makefile targets should auto-update when the Makefile changes? Or more like: a module README should reflect what's actually in that directory?\n\n2. \"Enforce changes in the format spec across the project\" — where does the format spec live? Is it a file in the repo (e.g. `specs/doc-format.md`) that the linter reads as its source of truth, or is the linter itself the spec?\n\n**Fix vs flag boundary:**\n\n3. What's your instinct on auto-fix scope? Safe fixes only (whitespace, heading levels, adding missing required sections as stubs) or more aggressive (reordering sections, rewriting cross-references)?\n\n4. When the sidecar flags something it can't fix — what's the output? A report file the LLM reads next iteration, stderr that blocks the loop, or both?\n\n**Integration:**\n\n5. Should this run as a pre-loop check (blocks `loop.sh` if it fails), a post-loop check (validates what the loop produced), or both?\n\n6. Is this a tool that ships inside unpossible 2 as a binary in the repo, or something installed separately (e.g. `go install`)?"

                "file_text": "# Doc Linter (doclint)\n\n## [1] Doc Linter\n\n- **Status:** ready\n- **Created:** 2026-03-31\n- **Promoted:**\n\n### Description\n\nA Go CLI binary (`doclint`) that validates, auto-fixes, and reports on markdown and README\nfiles across an unpossible project. Its job is to eliminate mechanical doc work so that\nLLM iterations only touch things requiring genuine reasoning.\n\nShips as a compiled binary inside the unpossible 2 repo under `tools/doclint/`. Runs as\na post-loop step in the Makefile. Reads its structural rules from a format spec file\n(`specs/doc-format.md`) so the rules evolve with the project without changing the binary.\n\n**Language: Go.** Single static binary, no runtime dependencies, fast enough to run after\nevery loop iteration without adding meaningful latency.\n\n**Core responsibilities:**\n\n1. **Validate structure** — check all markdown files against the format spec. Flag missing\n   required sections, incorrect heading hierarchy, files that exceed token budget thresholds.\n\n2. **Validate cross-references** — check that terms, section names, and file references used\n   in one doc resolve correctly in the docs they point to. Flag broken links and undefined\n   terms.\n\n3. **Sync checks** — detect drift between docs and their source of truth:\n   - README sections that list Makefile targets: flag when targets in the Makefile don't\n     match what the README documents\n   - Directory READMEs: flag when files present in the directory aren't mentioned and vice\n     versa (configurable per-directory)\n\n4. **Auto-fix (safe)** — apply without confirmation:\n   - Normalize heading levels to match the format spec\n   - Add missing required sections as stubs (e.g. `## Open Questions\\n\\n_None._`)\n   - Fix trailing whitespace, missing newlines at EOF\n   - Update auto-generated sections (Makefile target lists) from source of truth\n\n5. **Auto-fix (aggressive)** — require `--fix=aggressive` flag or interactive confirmation:\n   - Reorder sections to match the format spec sequence\n   - Rename sections to match canonical names in the format spec\n   - Rewrite cross-references when a referenced section has been renamed\n\n6. **LLM queue** — issues the binary cannot fix are written to `specs/doc-issues.md` in a\n   structured format. Each entry includes: file, line, issue type, and a plain-English\n   description of what needs resolving. The loop reads this file at the start of each\n   iteration and treats unresolved entries as tasks.\n\n**Architecture:**\n\n- `tools/doclint/main.go` — CLI entry point, flag parsing, exit codes\n- `tools/doclint/lint/` — rule engine: loads `specs/doc-format.md`, walks the file tree,\n  runs rules against each file\n- `tools/doclint/fix/` — fix engine: safe fixes applied automatically, aggressive fixes\n  gated by flag or prompt\n- `tools/doclint/report/` — writes `specs/doc-issues.md` for issues requiring LLM or\n  human resolution\n- `tools/doclint/sync/` — Makefile parser and directory scanner for drift detection\n\n**CLI interface:**\n\n```\ndoclint [flags] [path]\n\nFlags:\n  --fix             Apply safe fixes automatically (default: false)\n  --fix=aggressive  Apply safe + aggressive fixes (prompts for confirmation unless --yes)\n  --yes             Skip confirmation prompts (for use in loop/CI)\n  --format-spec     Path to format spec file (default: specs/doc-format.md)\n  --report          Path to issues output file (default: specs/doc-issues.md)\n  --quiet           Suppress stdout, only write report file\n```\n\nExit codes: `0` = clean or all issues fixed, `1` = unfixed issues remain, `2` = binary\nerror (bad config, unreadable files).\n\n**Format spec (`specs/doc-format.md`):**\n\nA markdown file that defines the rules doclint enforces. Sections:\n\n- `## Required Files` — list of files that must exist in the project\n- `## File Rules` — per-file or per-glob rules: required sections, section order, max tokens\n- `## Vocabulary` — terms that must be used consistently; aliases that should be normalized\n- `## Sync Rules` — which docs are derived from which sources (e.g. README target list ←\n  Makefile)\n\nThe format spec is itself validated by doclint on every run (bootstrapped rule: the spec\nmust contain all four required sections).\n\n**Makefile integration:**\n\n```makefile\nlint-docs:\n    @./tools/doclint/doclint --fix --report\n\nlint-docs-fix:\n    @./tools/doclint/doclint --fix=aggressive --yes --report\n```\n\n`lint-docs` runs automatically after each successful loop commit (added to the post-commit\nhook the loop manages). `lint-docs-fix` is a manual target for bulk cleanup.\n\n**LLM queue format (`specs/doc-issues.md`):**\n\n```markdown\n## Doc Issues\n\n<!-- doclint: auto-generated, do not edit manually -->\n\n- [ ] `specs/upload-photo.md` line 14: missing required section `## Acceptance Criteria`\n- [ ] `README.md` line 32: Makefile target `sb-sync` documented but not present in Makefile\n- [ ] `specs/prd.md`: term \"base image\" used here but \"docker image\" used in upload-photo.md — normalize to one\n```\n\nThe loop agent reads this file, resolves items it can, and checks them off. doclint removes\nchecked items on the next run.\n\n### Acceptance Criteria\n\n- `doclint` with no flags exits `1` and prints a summary when any rule in the format spec\n  is violated; exits `0` when the project is clean\n- `--fix` resolves all safe-fixable issues and re-runs validation; exits `0` if only\n  safe issues were present\n- Makefile drift detection catches at least: targets present in Makefile but missing from\n  README, and targets in README but removed from Makefile\n- Cross-reference validation catches broken internal links (`[text](other-file.md#section)`)\n  and undefined vocabulary terms\n- `specs/doc-issues.md` is written with one entry per unfixed issue in the format above;\n  resolved issues (checked off by the loop) are removed on the next run\n- The format spec itself is validated on every run; a malformed format spec exits `2` with\n  a clear error\n- Binary builds with `CGO_ENABLED=0` and produces a single static binary under 10MB\n\n### Open Questions\n\n- Should doclint watch for file changes and run incrementally (`--watch` mode) for use\n  during active spec writing in Zed, or is post-loop-only sufficient for now?\n- Should the Zed tasks.json include a `lint-docs` task, or is Makefile integration enough?\n",

The loop reads this file at the start of each\n   iteration and treats unresolved entries as tasks.\n\n**Architecture:**\n\n- `tools/doclint/main.go` — CLI entry point, flag parsing, exit codes\n- `tools/doclint/lint/` — rule engine: loads `specs/doc-format.md`, walks the file tree,\n  runs rules against each file\n- `tools/doclint/fix/` — fix engine: safe fixes applied automatically, aggressive fixes\n  gated by flag or prompt\n- `tools/doclint/report/` — writes `specs/doc-issues.md` for issues requiring LLM or\n  human resolution\n- `tools/doclint/sync/` — Makefile parser and directory scanner for drift detection\n\n**CLI interface:**\n\n```\ndoclint [flags] [path]\n\nFlags:\n  --fix             Apply safe fixes automatically (default: false)\n  --fix=aggressive  Apply safe + aggressive fixes (prompts for confirmation unless --yes)\n  --yes             Skip confirmation prompts (for use in loop/CI)\n  --format-spec     Path to format spec file (default: specs/doc-format.md)\n  --report          Path to issues output file (default: specs/doc-issues.md)\n  --quiet           Suppress stdout, only write report file\n```\n\nExit codes: `0` = clean or all issues fixed, `1` = unfixed issues remain, `2` = binary\nerror (bad config, unreadable files).\n\n**Format spec (`specs/doc-format.md`):**\n\nA markdown file that defines the rules doclint enforces. Sections:\n\n- `## Required Files` — list of files that must exist in the project\n- `## File Rules` — per-file or per-glob rules: required sections, section order, max tokens\n- `## Vocabulary` — terms that must be used consistently; aliases that should be normalized\n- `## Sync Rules` — which docs are derived from which sources (e.g. README target list ←\n  Makefile)\n\nThe format spec is itself validated by doclint on every run (bootstrapped rule: the spec\nmust contain all four required sections).\n\n**Makefile integration:**\n\n```makefile\nlint-docs:\n    @./tools/doclint/doclint --fix --report\n\nlint-docs-fix:\n    @./tools/doclint/doclint --fix=aggressive --yes --report\n```\n\n`lint-docs` runs automatically after each successful loop commit (added to the post-commit\nhook the loop manages). `lint-docs-fix` is a manual target for bulk cleanup.\n\n**LLM queue format (`specs/doc-issues.md`):**\n\n```markdown\n## Doc Issues\n\n<!-- doclint: auto-generated, do not edit manually -->\n\n- [ ] `specs/upload-photo.md` line 14: missing required section `## Acceptance Criteria`\n- [ ] `README.md` line 32: Makefile target `sb-sync` documented but not present in Makefile\n- [ ] `specs/prd.md`: term \"base image\" used here but \"docker image\" used in upload-photo.md — normalize to one\n```\n\nThe loop agent reads this file, resolves items it can, and checks them off. doclint removes\nchecked items on the next run.\n\n### Acceptance Criteria\n\n- `doclint` with no flags exits `1` and prints a summary when any rule in the format spec\n  is violated; exits `0` when the project is clean\n- `--fix` resolves all safe-fixable issues and re-runs validation; exits `0` if only\n  safe issues were present\n- Makefile drift detection catches at least: targets present in Makefile but missing from\n  README, and targets in README but removed from Makefile\n- Cross-reference validation catches broken internal links (`[text](other-file.md#section)`)\n  and undefined vocabulary terms\n- `specs/doc-issues.md` is written with one entry per unfixed issue in the format above;\n  resolved issues (checked off by the loop) are removed on the next run\n- The format spec itself is validated on every run; a malformed format spec exits `2` with\n  a clear error\n- Binary builds with `CGO_ENABLED=0` and produces a single static binary under 10MB\n\n### Open Questions\n\n- Should doclint watch for file changes and run incrementally (`--watch` mode) for use\n  during active spec writing in Zed, or is post-loop-only sufficient for now?\n- Should the Zed tasks.json include a `lint-docs` task, or is Makefile integration enough?\n",

          "content": "Good. The medium article is also in the context — I've read it. Key ideas worth integrating: grounding the PRD in user pain points before features, explicit MVP scope, and the \"detailed enough for a coding agent\" framing. That aligns exactly with what you're describing.\n\nLet me reflect back what I'm hearing on the PRD philosophy before drilling deeper:\n\n> A PRD is conceptual backpressure. It forces explicit choices about scope before the agent starts filling gaps with assumptions. The open questions section makes it safe to start incomplete — the PRD is a living document that gets sharper through iteration, not a gate that blocks work.\n\nThat's the right mental model. Now the hard questions:\n\n---\n\n**Round 2: Structure decisions**\n\n5. The Product School template has \"Change History\" — a log of who changed what and when. For a solo project with git, this is redundant. But for the \"shared understanding engine\" goal, a lightweight version (just a `## Status` field showing Draft → Review → Approved) might be useful as a signal to the loop about how much to trust the spec. Do you want a status field, or does git history cover it?\n\n6. \"Messaging\" in the Product School template means how marketing describes the product. Irrelevant for internal tools. But there's a related concept that *is* relevant for agent interfaces: **the one-sentence description of what this feature does and why it exists**, written so an agent reading it cold understands the intent without reading the whole doc. Do you want a mandatory \"Intent\" or \"Purpose\" field at the top — one or two sentences max?\n\n7. The medium article recommends explicit **personas** before user stories. For unpossible 2, the personas are: you (solo developer), future contributors, and the loop agent itself. The agent-as-persona is unusual but important — it has different needs than a human reader (needs unambiguous acceptance criteria, explicit scope boundaries, no implicit knowledge). Do you want a lightweight personas section, or is \"the audience is always the same\" a reason to skip it?\n\n8. The article recommends **success metrics** — measurable outcomes post-launch. For internal tooling this often gets skipped, but for the loop it's actually useful: if the PRD says \"success = loop completes task manager feature in under 10 iterations\", that's a constraint the agent can use to self-evaluate. Do you want success metrics, or is acceptance criteria sufficient?\n\n9. You mentioned a **PRD revision cycle** as a TODO. My instinct: the open questions section already enables iteration, but a revision cycle needs a trigger — something that says \"this PRD needs updating\". Options: the loop flags it when it hits an ambiguity, doclint flags it when acceptance criteria are all checked off, or you manually promote it. Which trigger makes sense?\n\n---\n\n**Round 3: Agent interface specifics**\n\n10. The medium article emphasizes \"detailed enough for a coding agent.\" In your experience with the loop so far, what's the most common thing the agent gets wrong that a better PRD would have prevented — missing a constraint, misunderstanding scope, or implementing the wrong thing entirely?\n\n11. Should the PRD explicitly list **what the agent is allowed to create** (files, directories, external dependencies) as a constraint section? This is the \"features out\" concept but applied to implementation decisions, not product scope."

                "file_text": "---\nname: write-a-prd\ncommand: make write-a-prd\ndescription: Turn a grilled idea into a PRD file with user stories\nmodel: opus\nloop_type: plan\nprinciples: [planning]\n---\n\nProduce a Product Requirements Document from a spec. Follow these steps in order, skipping\nany step already completed in the current conversation:\n\n1. **Locate the spec** — ask the user which spec file to use, or accept it as an argument.\n   Read it fully.\n2. **Check for existing PRD** — look for a file named `[spec-name]-prd.md` co-located with\n   the spec. If one exists, show the user its Status field and current sections, then ask:\n   \"This PRD already exists — are we revising it or starting fresh?\" Do not proceed until\n   confirmed.\n3. **Explore the codebase** — verify any assertions in the spec against existing code. Note\n   gaps or contradictions.\n4. **Interview** — if shared understanding has not been reached, run the grill-me process\n   before continuing.\n5. **Sketch major modules** — identify the key components needed to fulfill the spec. Note\n   integration points and unknowns.\n6. **Write the PRD** — produce or update `[spec-name]-prd.md` using the template below.\n\n## PRD Template\n\n```markdown\n# PRD: [Feature Name]\n\n- **Status:** Draft\n- **Created:** [date]\n- **Last revised:** [date]\n\n## Intent\nOne or two sentences. What this feature is and why it exists — written so an agent reading\nthis document cold understands the purpose without reading further.\n\n## Personas\nWho uses this feature and what they need from it. For internal unpossible features use the\ndefaults below; replace with grill-me output for client projects.\n\n**Default personas:**\n- **Solo developer (you):** building and iterating on unpossible projects; needs clarity and\n  speed\n- **Future contributor:** onboarding to an existing project; needs shared understanding\n  without tribal knowledge\n- **Loop agent:** executing tasks autonomously; needs unambiguous scope and explicit\n  boundaries\n\n## User Scenarios\nFull stories describing how each persona uses the feature in context. Not bullet points —\nnarrative paragraphs that show the before, during, and after.\n\n## User Stories\n- As a [persona], I want [action] so that [outcome].\n\n## Success Metrics\nMeasurable outcomes that indicate this feature is working. The agent should suggest\ncandidates based on the feature type if the author hasn't defined them yet.\n\n| Goal | Metric |\n|---|---|\n| [goal] | [measurable indicator] |\n\n## Functional Requirements\nWhat the feature does. Describe inputs, processes, and expected outputs per capability.\nMark the MVP boundary explicitly — everything above the line ships first.\n\n**MVP:**\n- ...\n\n**Post-MVP:**\n- ...\n\n## Features Out\nWhat this PRD explicitly does not cover, and why. Be specific — vague exclusions get\nignored.\n\n- ...\n\n## Designs\nLinks to wireframes, mockups, or interaction specs. Placeholder until assets exist.\n\n- Links to Wireframes/Mockups: _none yet_\n\n## Open Questions\nUnresolved decisions that will affect implementation. Add entries freely — open questions\nmake it safe to start a PRD before everything is known. Resolve or defer each before\npromoting Status to Approved.\n\n| Question | Answer | Date |\n|---|---|---|\n| [question] | | |\n```\n\nWrite the file to disk. Do not create any tasks or modify any other files.\n",

                    "Text": "=== Operation 1 Result (Text) ===\n# General Coding Practices\n\nLoaded every build iteration. Language-agnostic rules that apply to all code written in this project.\n\n## Comments\n- Explain *why*, never *what* — if a comment restates the code, delete it\n- A function that needs a comment to describe what it does should be renamed or split\n- Mark non-obvious decisions: `// must happen before X because Y`\n- TODO comments must include a reason: `// TODO: replace once Z is resolved`\n\n## Naming\n- Self-documenting names — a reader should know what a thing does without opening it\n- No single-letter variables outside loop counters\n- No abbreviations unless domain-standard (e.g. `ctx`, `err`, `id` are fine; `usrMgr` is not)\n- Booleans read as assertions: `isReady`, `hasError`, `canRetry`\n\n## Functions\n- Single responsibility — one function does one thing\n- If you need a comment to summarize what a function does, split or rename it\n- Prefer short functions; deep nesting is a sign of missing extraction\n- Side effects should be obvious from the name or signature\n\n## Files\n- One concept per file\n- Filename should make its contents guessable without opening it\n- Group files by feature or domain, not by type (e.g. `user/` not `models/`, `handlers/`, `utils/`)\n\n## Dead Code\n- Delete it — never comment it out\n- If it might be needed later, that's what git is for\n\n## Error Handling\n- Explicit and visible — no silent swallows\n- Errors should propagate with context so the call site can understand what failed\n- Don't log *and* return an error — pick one\n\n## Dependencies\n- Prefer stdlib\n- Add a package only when the benefit clearly outweighs the cost of the dependency\n- Pin versions explicitly\n\n## General\n- No magic numbers — name constants\n- Fail fast: validate inputs at the boundary, trust internals\n- Consistency beats cleverness — follow the patterns already in the codebase\n\n=== Operation 2 Result (Text) ===\n# General Planning Practices\n\nLoaded every plan iteration. Guides how to analyze specs and produce an implementation plan.\n\n## Spec Granularity: Activities, Not Capabilities\n\nEach spec file should cover exactly one **activity** — a verb in the user journey, not a system\ncapability. Use the **One Sentence Without \"And\"** test:\n\n- ✓ \"The user uploads a photo\" → `upload-photo.md`\n- ✗ \"The image system handles upload, storage, and processing\" → 3 specs\n\nActivities are naturally scoped by user intent. Capability-named specs tend to grow unbounded.\n\nIf `projects/<name>/specs/audience.md` exists, the activity names and their capability depths are defined there —\nuse them as the authoritative list of spec topics.\n\n## SLC Release Scoping\n\nIf `projects/<name>/specs/audience.md` exists and defines a **Current target release**, scope\n`projects/<name>/IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` to that release only — not the full feature space.\n\nA good release slice is:\n- **Simple** — narrow enough to ship in a focused build loop\n- **Lovable** — delivers real value to the target audience within its scope\n- **Complete** — fully accomplishes a job; no broken preview states\n\nDo not plan tasks for capability depths beyond the current target release. Tasks for future\nreleases belong in a future planning pass, not the current plan.\n\n## Acceptance-Driven Task Definition\n\nFor each task, derive required tests from the acceptance criteria in the relevant spec:\n\n- Acceptance criteria describe *what* to verify (observable outcomes)\n- Required tests are the verification points derived from those criteria\n- Include required tests in the task definition in `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md`\n\nExample task entry:\n```\n- [ ] Extract dominant colors from uploaded image (app/extract.py)\n  Required tests: returns 5–10 colors, handles grayscale, handles single-color image, <100ms for <5MB\n```\n\nThis prevents tasks from being marked done without the required verification existing and passing.\n\n## Gap Analysis\n- Read `projects/<name>/src/` before declaring anything missing — never assume unimplemented\n- Compare against each spec file in `projects/<name>/specs/` explicitly, item by item\n- Flag `projects/<name>/infra/Dockerfile` and `projects/<name>/infra/docker-compose.yml` placeholder values as high-priority\n\n## Task Structure\n- Each task in `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` must be independently verifiable\n- Tasks should be small enough to complete and test in one loop iteration\n- Order by dependency — unblock other tasks first\n- Distinguish clearly between \"not started\", \"partially done\", and \"done but untested\"\n\n## Specs Integrity\n- If specs contradict each other, resolve the conflict before planning implementation\n- If a requirement is ambiguous, make the ambiguity explicit in `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md`\n- Never invent requirements — if something seems missing, flag it, don't add it silently\n\n## Development Phases\n\nNew projects advance through phases. Each phase must be stable before the next begins.\nThe current phase is recorded in `specs/prd.md` under `## Phase`. Default to Phase 0 for new projects.\n\n| Phase | Name | Infrastructure | Goal |\n|---|---|---|---|\n| 0 | Local | `docker-compose up`, `docker-compose run test` | App runs and tests pass on dev machine |\n| 1 | CI | GitHub Actions (or equivalent) | Tests run automatically on every push |\n| 2 | Staging | Remote deploy (platform-specific) | App reachable at a non-production URL |\n| 3 | Production | Multi-env, secrets mgmt, monitoring | Production-ready, security hardened |\n\n**Phase rules:**\n- Plan tasks only for the current phase. Future-phase infrastructure (k8s, secrets managers, staging pipelines) must not appear in the implementation plan until that phase is active.\n- To advance a phase, add a task: `[ ] Advance to Phase N` — it requires the previous phase's acceptance criteria to all be passing.\n- When planning Phase 0, the only infra files needed are `infra/Dockerfile` and `infra/docker-compose.yml`.\n- When planning Phase 1, add CI config (e.g. `.github/workflows/ci.yml`). Do not add deploy jobs yet.\n- When planning Phase 2, add a deploy workflow and a staging environment. Do not add production config yet.\n- When planning Phase 3, add production config, secrets management, and security hardening.\n\n## IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md\n- It is the agent's only memory across context windows — keep it current\n- Remove completed items when the file grows large (>50 items)\n- Each item should name the files it will touch, so future iterations can target searches\n- The plan is disposable state — regenerate it entirely when it's stale or wrong\n\n=== Operation 3 Result (Text) ===\n# General Verification Practices\n\nLoaded when running tests. Applies regardless of language or framework.\n\n## Backpressure\n\nTests, typechecks, lints, and builds are not just quality gates — they are the mechanism that\nprevents the loop from marking work done when it isn't. A task is not complete until the full\nvalidation suite passes. This is called **acceptance-driven backpressure**: the agent cannot\nmove forward until reality agrees with the spec.\n\nWithout strong backpressure the loop produces the appearance of progress rather than actual\nprogress. Every skipped test or suppressed error is a hole in that pressure.\n\n### LLM-as-Judge\nFor criteria that can't be expressed as a traditional test (tone, visual layout, subjective\nquality), write an LLM-as-judge check: a prompt that evaluates output against the spec and\nreturns pass/fail. Treat it as a first-class test — it must pass before committing, just like\nany other assertion.\n\n## Before Running Tests\n- Rebuild the Docker image if dependencies or the Dockerfile changed\n- Run the full test suite, not just tests for the code you touched\n- If unrelated tests fail, fix them — don't skip or suppress\n\n## What to Test\n- Test behavior, not implementation — tests should survive refactoring\n- Cover: happy path, edge cases, invalid input, error conditions\n- Each test should have one reason to fail\n- Test names should read as sentences describing the expected behavior\n\n## What Not to Test\n- Third-party library internals\n- Trivial pass-through code with no logic\n- Code that is about to be deleted\n\n## On Failure\n- Read the full error output before changing anything\n- Reproduce the failure with the smallest possible input\n- Fix the root cause — don't mask the failure with a broader catch or a skip\n- If a test is genuinely wrong (not the code), fix the test and document why\n\n## After Tests Pass\n- Commit immediately — don't bundle multiple task completions into one commit\n- Tag if this brings the build to a fully green state (increment patch: 0.0.1, 0.0.2, ...)\n- Update `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` to mark the task complete\n\n=== Operation 4 Result (Text) ===\n# Zed Editor Practices\n\nPrinciples for using Zed effectively in the unpossible workflow. The goal is to keep\nthe loop running in the editor, review its output without leaving Zed, and write better\nspecs before the loop ever starts.\n\n## Core Setup\n\nAdd to your Zed `settings.json` (cmd+shift+p → \"zed: open settings\"):\n\n```json\n\"git\": {\n  \"inline_blame\": {\n    \"enabled\": true\n  }\n},\n\"terminal\": {\n  \"shell\": \"system\"\n}\n```\n\nInline blame shows who (or which loop iteration) last touched each line without opening\nthe git panel. Essential for reviewing loop output in spec files.\n\n## Running the Loop from Zed\n\nUse tasks instead of typing commands manually. Open with `cmd+shift+p` → \"task: spawn\".\n\n| Task | When to use |\n|---|---|\n| `loop: build (single, streaming)` | Reviewing one iteration live — output stays visible |\n| `loop: plan (single, streaming)` | Gap analysis before a build run — watch Claude's reasoning |\n| `loop: build (background)` | Letting the loop run unattended — terminal opens but doesn't steal focus |\n| `loop: plan (background)` | Same, for plan mode |\n| `sandbox: shell` | Dropping into the Docker sandbox for manual inspection |\n\nTasks are defined in `.zed/tasks.json` at the project root. Mirror any new Makefile\ntargets there — don't add tasks for things not already in the Makefile.\n\n## Terminal Layout\n\nZed's terminal panel (`cmd+j` to toggle) is sufficient for most loop work. For a\nnear-fullscreen terminal without leaving Zed:\n\n- Drag the terminal divider up until only the tab bar is visible above it\n- Or open a second Zed window (`cmd+shift+n`) dedicated to the running loop — one window\n  for specs/code, one for terminal output\n\nAvoid switching to iTerm mid-session. Context switching between apps breaks the review\nflow that Zed's split panes enable.\n\n## Reviewing Loop Output (Git)\n\nAfter each loop commit, review in Zed before touching anything else:\n\n1. `cmd+shift+g` opens the git panel — see all changed files at a glance\n2. Click any file in the panel to open its diff inline\n3. Gutter indicators (colored bars on the left edge) show changed lines in every open file\n4. To revert a single hunk: click the gutter indicator → \"Revert Hunk\"\n5. To revert the entire last commit: open the terminal and run `git reset --hard HEAD~1`\n\nDo not open the GitHub web UI to review loop commits. Everything you need is in the\ngit panel. The web UI is for sharing and PRs, not for iteration review.\n\n## Spec Writing Workflow\n\nThe most common cause of unintended loop behavior is specs that contradict each other\nor leave gaps the loop fills with assumptions. Zed's project search is the fix.\n\n**Before running the loop:**\n\n1. Open all active spec files in a split pane: `specs/prd.md` left, the relevant\n   activity spec right\n2. Use `cmd+shift+f` (project search) to search for any feature name or term you're\n   about to implement — verify it appears consistently across all spec files\n3. Look for the same term meaning different things in different files — that's the gap\n   the loop will exploit\n\n**Split pane layout for spec work:**\n- Left pane: `specs/prd.md` (constraints — language, framework, port)\n- Right pane: the activity spec you're currently writing\n- Terminal panel (collapsed): available via `cmd+j` when you need to run `plan1` to\n  check Claude's interpretation\n\n## Working Across Template and Project Simultaneously\n\nWhen porting improvements from unpossible (the template) to a project built from it:\n\n1. `File → Add Folder to Project` — add both roots to one Zed workspace\n2. Use `cmd+shift+f` to search across both — find where a practice or prompt pattern\n   needs updating in the project\n3. Use split panes to edit the template file and the project file side by side\n\nThis replaces the window-switching workflow and makes diffs between template and project\nvisible without a separate tool.\n\n## What Not to Do in Zed\n\n- Don't use the Zed agent panel for loop work — it's a separate context from the loop\n  and will confuse your mental model of what the agent knows\n- Don't run `loop.sh` directly in the terminal when a task exists for it — tasks keep\n  the command consistent and documented\n- Don't review loop output in the GitHub web UI during active development — it breaks\n  the edit→run→review cycle that Zed's git panel supports natively\n\n=== Operation 5 Result (Text) ===\n# Testing Principals\n\n## Core Principles\n\n- **Test behavior, not implementation.** Tests should verify what the code does, not how it does it.\n- **One assertion per test** where possible. Multiple assertions signal multiple behaviors that should be split.\n- **Test all cases.** For each unit of behavior, cover: happy path, edge cases, and error/invalid input.\n- **Prefer real dependencies over mocks** for integration tests. Mock only at system boundaries (external APIs, file system, clock).\n- **Keep tests fast.** Slow tests break the development loop. Isolate slow tests (DB, network) so unit tests can run quickly.\n- **Tests are documentation.** A failing test should tell you exactly what broke and why.\n\n## Test Categories\n\n### Unit Tests\n- Test individual functions/modules in isolation.\n- Fast, no I/O, no external dependencies.\n- Cover edge cases and error conditions.\n\n### Integration Tests\n- Test how components work together.\n- May use real databases, file system, etc.\n- Slower — run separately from unit tests if needed.\n\n### End-to-End Tests\n- Test the full application flow from the user's perspective.\n- Fewest in number, highest value for regressions.\n\n## What NOT to Test\n\n- Third-party library internals.\n- Trivial getters/setters with no logic.\n- Code that is about to be deleted."

                "content": [
                  {
                    "Text": "=== Operation 1 Result (Text) ===\n# Tasks Module\n\n## What It Does\n\nOwns the agent execution config that drives loop behaviour. A `Task` record is the machine-facing representation of a `Story` with `item_type: work_item` — it extends the Story with fields the LLM runtime needs: which tools are available, which model to use, which prompt template to apply. Parses `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` into structured Task records after each plan loop.\n\n## Why It Exists\n\nStories track human-facing intent and status. Tasks track agent execution config. They are the same unit of work viewed from two angles — a Task always has a corresponding Story record (`story_id` FK). Keeping execution config in the Task schema makes tool selection and model choice deterministic — controlled by the system, not the agent.\n\n## Relationship to Stories\n\n- Every Task has a `story_id` pointing to a Story with `item_type: work_item`\n- Creating a Task upserts the corresponding Story record\n- Status transitions on a Task propagate to the Story (and trigger the spec file header rewrite via the Stories sync)\n- Assigning a Task to an `AgentProfile` sets the Story's `assignee_type: agent` and `assignee_id`\n\n## Task Schema\n\nEach task record stores:\n- `id` — UUID primary key\n- `story_id` — FK to the corresponding Story record (item_type: work_item)\n- `title` and `description` — from the plan checkbox\n- `status` — pending / in_progress / complete / failed / blocked (mirrors Story status)\n- `loop_type` — plan / build / review / reflect / research\n- `provider` and `model` — which LLM to use (overridable per task)\n- `prompt_template` — provider-tailored prompt (nullable — falls back to `PROMPT_{mode}.md`)\n- `reviewer_provider` and `reviewer_model` — for the producer/reviewer pattern\n- `allowed_tools` — list of tool names the agent may use for this task\n- `task_ref` — stable identifier derived from the checkbox text (SHA256)\n- `depends_on_ids` — list of task refs this task depends on\n\n## Plan Parsing\n\nA plan parser job runs after each plan loop completes. It:\n1. Reads `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` from the configured workspace path\n2. Parses `- [ ]` (pending) and `- [x]` (complete) checkboxes\n3. Upserts task records keyed on `task_ref`\n4. Preserves manually set `provider`/`model` overrides — does not overwrite them on re-parse\n\nMD remains the source of truth. The database is the query layer.\n\n## Promote Flow\n\n`POST /api/tasks/:id/promote` advances a task to the next stage. The shell command `./loop.sh promote <id>` is a thin wrapper calling this endpoint.\n\n## Acceptance Criteria\n\n- Unchecked plan item → task with status: pending\n- Checked plan item → task with status: complete\n- Re-running the plan parser is idempotent\n- Manually set provider/model on a task is not overwritten by re-parse\n- Malformed `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` logs a warning and continues — does not raise\n- `GET /api/tasks` filters by status and loop_type\n- `POST /api/tasks/:id/promote` changes status to in_progress\n- Unauthenticated requests return 401\n\n=== Operation 2 Result (Text) ===\n# Analytics — System\n\n## What It Does\n\nCaptures LLM, infrastructure, and loop signal from every agent iteration. Stores it in Postgres and surfaces it through a queryable API. Feeds the Reflect loop with cost and error patterns for self-improvement.\n\n## Why Build It\n\n- Feed the Reflect loop — LLM cost and error patterns drive self-improvement\n- Own the data — no third-party service receives it\n- Ops visibility — loop run counts, failure rates, rollback events\n\n## Ingest Architecture\n\nThe ingest path must be:\n- **Non-blocking** — a slow analytics write must never slow down the main application\n- **Always available** — if the app is restarting, events should still be accepted and buffered\n- **High-throughput** — LLM metrics fire on every agent iteration\n\nA dedicated ingest process with an in-memory queue and batch writes satisfies all three. The application calls the ingest endpoint fire-and-forget. The ingest process owns only capture and flush — the application owns all reads and the query API.\n\n## Signal Categories\n\n### LLM metrics\nPer agent run: provider, model, input tokens, output tokens, cost estimate, task type, duration.\n```\nevent_name: \"llm.run_completed\"\nproperties: { provider, model, input_tokens, output_tokens, cost_usd, mode, task_id, duration_ms }\ndistinct_id: <run_id>\n```\n\n### Infrastructure metrics\nLoop run counts, failure rates, rollback events, container dispatch results.\n```\nevent_name: \"loop.iteration_completed\"\nproperties: { mode, exit_code, duration_ms, agent, model }\nevent_name: \"loop.rollback_triggered\"\nproperties: { mode, iteration, reason }\n```\n\n## Audit Log\n\nSeparate concern from analytics. Analytics is for operational insight; audit is for compliance and security. They may share a database but nothing else.\n\n## Query API\n\nThe application server owns all reads. The ingest process never exposes query endpoints.\n\n```\nGET /api/analytics/llm     — aggregate cost/tokens by provider/model/date\nGET /api/analytics/loops   — run counts, failure rates by mode\nGET /api/analytics/summary — total cost this week, tasks completed, loop error rate\n```\n\nAll endpoints require authentication.\n\n## PII & Security\n\n- `distinct_id` is always an opaque UUID — never an email address or name\n- `properties` are filtered through secret/PII redaction patterns before storage\n- The ingest endpoint is internal-only — never exposed publicly\n\n## Phase 0 Scope\n\n- Ingest: `/capture` endpoint, in-memory queue, batch flush\n- `analytics_events` table\n- Query API: `/llm`, `/loops`, `/summary`\n- Dashboard: LLM cost panel and loop success rate panel\n\n## Acceptance Criteria\n\n- `POST /capture` returns 202 immediately regardless of flush state\n- Events flushed within 5 seconds or after 100 events, whichever comes first\n- Events buffered in memory if storage temporarily unavailable — no events dropped\n- `GET /api/analytics/llm` returns cost aggregated by provider/model, filterable by date range\n- `GET /api/analytics/loops` returns run counts and failure rates by mode\n- `GET /api/analytics/summary` returns total cost this week, tasks completed, loop error rate\n- Ingest endpoint is not reachable from outside the internal network"

          "content": "Good answers. Capturing the glossary note — I'll add that to the README after the PRD. More questions:\n\n**Round 2: The hard edges**\n\n6. \"Tasks either print their backlog to disk or allow the agent to query the API directly\" — this is a fork in the architecture. Printing to disk means the loop reads a file (like it reads `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` today). Querying the API means the loop makes HTTP calls. Which does the loop currently do, and which do you *want* it to do in unpossible 2?\n\n7. Dependency enforcement: if Task B depends on Task A, and Task A is `in_progress` but not `complete`, promoting Task B should return what — a 422 with a clear error message listing the blocking tasks? And does this apply to the `prd-to-tasks` skill too, or only at runtime?\n\n8. The plan parser runs \"after each plan loop completes.\" Who triggers it — the loop itself calling the API, a background job polling for file changes, or something else?     "Good, I have enough to work with. Let me give you the Zed advantages list filtered by highest productivity gain for your specific situation — then we'll use this to shape the practice doc.\n\n---\n\n## Zed Features Worth Your Attention (ranked by impact for your workflow)\n\n**1. Terminal panel toggle + zoom — solves your fullscreen problem**\n`cmd+j` toggles the terminal. But the real find: `cmd+shift+f` won't help here — what you want is Zed's panel \"expand\" behavior. You can drag the terminal divider up to near-fullscreen, and Zed remembers it. Even better: open a second Zed window dedicated to the terminal via `cmd+shift+n` and run `loop.sh` there. Two windows, one for code/specs, one for the loop. On a 16\" laptop this beats iTerm context switching.\n\n**2. Built-in git diff and blame — your GitLens replacement**\nZed has:\n- Gutter indicators (colored bars) showing changed/added/deleted lines inline\n- `cmd+shift+g` opens the git panel — staged/unstaged changes, commit message, commit button\n- Click any gutter indicator to see the inline diff hunk right there\n- `git blame` per line via hovering (enable with `\"git\": {\"inline_blame\": {\"enabled\": true}}` in settings)\n\nThis means after each loop iteration you can review exactly what changed without leaving Zed or opening GitHub. You can also revert individual hunks from the gutter.\n\n**3. Multi-buffer / project search for spec review**\n`cmd+shift+f` (project search) lets you search across all your spec files simultaneously. Critical use case: before running the loop, search for ambiguous terms or feature names across all `specs/*.md` files to catch contradictions. This is the \"read all the specs\" problem you identified — searching forces you to see cross-file consistency.\n\n**4. Multibuffer editing — edit specs across files in one view**\n`cmd+shift+p` → \"project symbols\" or use `cmd+shift+f` and open results as a multibuffer. You can edit multiple spec files side by side in a single buffer. Useful when you're aligning `prd.md`, `plan.md`, and an activity spec so they don't contradict each other.\n\n**5. Inline edit predictions (genuinely free)**\nYour `edit_predictions: \"zed\"` setting is already on. Zed's own model predicts completions as you type in any file including markdown. When writing specs, this is free autocomplete that learns your patterns. Not as powerful as Claude but zero cost and zero latency.\n\n**6. Tasks — run loop.sh without leaving Zed**\nZed has a built-in task runner. You define tasks in `.zed/tasks.json` and run them via `cmd+shift+p` → \"task: spawn\". You could define `loop.sh`, `loop.sh plan 1`, `loop.sh 1` as named tasks and run them from the command palette. Output goes to Zed's terminal panel. This is the integration point between Zed and the loop you're missing.\n\n**7. Workspace with multiple projects**\n`File → Add Folder to Project` adds a second root to your current workspace. You can have `unpossible` (the template) and `my-project` (built from it) open simultaneously, search across both, and copy/diff between them. Saves the constant window switching when porting improvements.\n\n---\n\n**The three that will change your day-to-day most:**\n- Tasks (6) — run the loop from inside Zed, output stays in context\n- Git panel + gutter diffs (2) — review loop commits without GitHub web UI\n- Project search before running the loop (3) — catch spec contradictions before they become unintended implementations\n\n---\n\nBefore I write the practice doc: does the Tasks feature resonate as the centerpiece? It's the tightest integration point — a `.zed/tasks.json` that ships with unpossible 2 would mean anyone using the template gets the loop wired into their editor out of the box. That feels like the highest-leverage thing to standardize.\n[Tool uses: none]",

    "> I want to develop a custom linter for md and readme files created for unpossible 2. suggest vialbe paths (go sidecar) extingind an existing linter etc",
    "Good problem. Here are the viable paths, ranked by fit for your situation:\n\n---\n\n**1. Extend markdownlint (Node.js custom rules)**\nmarkdownlint-cli2 supports custom rule plugins as plain JS files. You write rules that check for unpossible-specific patterns — required sections in spec files, forbidden phrases, token budget warnings, etc. Runs via `npx markdownlint-cli2` or a Makefile target. Zed has a markdownlint extension that picks up custom rules automatically.\n\n- Pro: zero infrastructure, rules are ~20 lines each, huge existing rule library to build on\n- Con: Node dependency, rules are structural not semantic (can't catch \"this spec contradicts that one\")\n- Best for: enforcing spec file shape (required headings, max line counts, forbidden patterns)\n\n**2. Go sidecar binary**\nA small Go CLI that takes a file or directory and emits lint errors in a format Zed understands (LSP diagnostics or just stdout). You own the rules entirely. Ships as a single binary alongside unpossible 2.\n\n- Pro: no runtime dependency for users, fast, can do cross-file analysis (catch contradictions between `prd.md` and activity specs), fits the \"ships with the template\" model cleanly\n- Con: you have to build and maintain it, more upfront work\n- Best for: the cross-file contradiction detection that markdownlint can't do\n\n**3. Vale**\nVale is a prose linter with a YAML-based rule system. You write styles (vocabularies, patterns, tone rules) in YAML. Has a Zed extension. Used by a lot of technical doc projects.\n\n- Pro: no code, just YAML config, handles vocabulary/tone rules well, active project\n- Con: rule expressiveness is limited to pattern matching and style — can't do structural spec validation, another binary to install\n- Best for: enforcing writing style in spec files (avoid vague words, flag \"TBD\", enforce JTBD language)\n\n**4. Combine Vale + markdownlint**\nVale for prose/vocabulary rules, markdownlint for structure. Both have Zed extensions and can run as a pre-loop check in the Makefile.\n\n- Pro: covers both dimensions without writing code\n- Con: two tools to configure and keep in sync, neither does cross-file analysis\n\n---\n\n**My read on your situation:**\n\nThe contradiction-between-specs problem you described is the highest-value thing to catch. That's cross-file semantic analysis — only the Go sidecar can do it properly. But it's also the most work.\n\nA pragmatic path: start with **markdownlint custom rules** for structure enforcement (required sections, max file size, forbidden phrases like \"TBD\" or \"etc\") and add a **Go sidecar** later specifically for cross-file validation. The markdownlint rules ship in days, the Go tool ships when you need it.\n\nBefore I go further — what kinds of rules matter most to you? Structure (required headings, file length), vocabulary (banned words, required JTBD framing), or cross-file consistency (term used in prd.md must appear in activity spec)?\n[Tool uses: none]",

    "> 1. we wnat the system to be adaptable so we need tasks to either print their backlog to disk or if its more efficient allow the agent to query the tasks api directly> \n> \n2.     "On question 10 — ditching `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` entirely is the right long-term direction but has one risk: the loop currently uses it as its only memory across context windows. If you remove it before the task API is fully reliable, the loop has nothing to orient from. My recommendation: keep `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` as a read-only snapshot that the task API *generates* on demand (like a print-to-disk export), not as the source of truth. The API owns the data, the file is just a human-readable view. That way the loop can still read a file if the API is down or not yet built.\n\nCapturing that as a decision.     "> 11.     "Good. That's a clean architecture. Let me reflect the full picture back before writing the PRD:\n\n---\n\n**Shared understanding — Task Manager**\n\n**What it is:** The execution layer between human intent (specs/PRDs) and the loop. A Task is a unit of agent work with enough config for the loop to run it autonomously.\n\n**Source of truth:** The task API owns task state. `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` becomes a per-feature file — a local log of tasks for that feature. New items added to it (by loop or human) are synced to the API as new tasks. The API can export a snapshot back to disk on demand.\n\n**Loop interaction model:** The loop calls `GET /api/tasks/next` (or similar) to receive all unblocked pending tasks with descriptions, picks one, works on it, then PATCHes status via the API. No more reading a flat checkbox list.\n\n**Dependency enforcement:** Promoting a task to `in_progress` returns 422 if any `depends_on` tasks are not `complete`. Creating tasks (via `prd-to-tasks` or plan file sync) is never blocked by dependencies — only execution is.\n\n**AgentProfile:** Config record for an LLM agent — provider, model, allowed tools, prompt template. A Task is assigned to an AgentProfile to determine how it runs. Named profiles like \"planner\" and \"builder\" are valid.\n\n**Plan file sync:** New `- [ ]` items added to a feature's plan file trigger task creation via a `POST /api/tasks/sync` endpoint. Existing tasks are not duplicated (keyed on `task_ref`). Edited checkbox text creates a new task — the old one is not deleted, just orphaned (flagged in the API response).\n\n**Story sync:** Task status changes propagate to the corresponding Story record synchronously. Spec file header rewrite is out of Phase 0 scope — DB propagation only.\n\n**Phase 0 scope:** Ingest (plan file sync endpoint), task CRUD, `GET /api/tasks/next`, promote flow with dependency enforcement, AgentProfile assignment, Story propagation.\n\n**Out of scope for Phase 0:** Spec file header rewrite, `IMPLEMENTATION_PLAN.md` export endpoint, multi-agent parallelism.\n\n---\n\nDoes this match? Any corrections before I write the PRD?\n[Tool uses: none]",
